[{"idx_paper": "10.1109/TKDE.2013.10", "q": "10.1109/TKDE.2013.10", "items": [{"id": "Dk7xgBdtsvc", "statistics": {"favoriteCount": "0", "commentCount": "0", "viewCount": "1878", "dislikeCount": "11", "likeCount": "1"}, "snippet": {"channelId": "UCV63FScHnShoobfaENC8R_Q", "title": "Nearest neighbor search", "description": "Nearest neighbor search (NNS), also known as proximity search, similarity search or closest point search, is an optimization problem for finding closest (or most similar) points. Closeness is typically expressed in terms of a dissimilarity function: The less similar are the objects, the larger are the function values. Formally, the nearest-neighbor (NN) search problem is defined as follows: given a set S of points in a space M and a query point q\u00a0\u2208\u00a0M, find the closest point in S to q. Donald Knuth in vol. 3 of The Art of Computer Programming (1973) called it the post-office problem, referring to an application of assigning to a residence the nearest post office. A direct generalization of this problem is a k-NN search, where we need to find the k closest points.\nMost commonly M is a metric space and dissimilarity is expressed as a distance metric, which is symmetric and satisfies the triangle inequality. Even more common, M is taken to be the d-dimensional vector space where dissimilarity is measured using the Euclidean distance, Manhattan distance or other distance metric. However, the dissimilarity function can be arbitrary. One example are asymmetric Bregman divergences, for which the triangle inequality does not hold.\n\n\nThis video is targeted to blind users.\n\nAttribution:\nArticle text available under CC-BY-SA\nCreative Commons image source in video", "publishedAt": "2014-11-15T04:03:49Z", "channelTitle": "Audiopedia"}, "contentDetails": {"duration": "PT20M11S"}}]}, {"idx_paper": "10.1109/TKDE.2013.12", "q": "10.1109/TKDE.2013.12", "items": [{"id": "VOOaZE2N8Zs", "statistics": {"favoriteCount": "0", "commentCount": "1", "viewCount": "3002", "dislikeCount": "0", "likeCount": "13"}, "snippet": {"channelId": "UCD4OarBUVn-txH6zKn79iqg", "description": "R/V Sikuliaq return to the  Menominee River 23 Feb 2014 from her acceptance sea trials  Ludington, MI", "tags": ["ships", "Ice", "Breaker", "Science", "NSF", "Great", "Lakes"], "title": "RV Sikuliaq return from Acceptance Trials", "publishedAt": "2014-02-24T03:57:35Z", "channelTitle": "Scott Hartz"}, "contentDetails": {"duration": "PT2M6S"}}]}, {"idx_paper": "10.1109/TPDS.2013.49", "q": "10.1109/TPDS.2013.49", "items": [{"id": "HecG-T0x2hM", "statistics": {"favoriteCount": "0", "viewCount": "1633", "dislikeCount": "1", "likeCount": "7"}, "snippet": {"channelId": "UCUdJZSkKOQPpoDyqPz-lSjA", "description": "The chemistry department at Kansas State University offers a 10-week REU summer research program, which gives undergraduate students the opportunity to perform important research in a laboratory setting. However, SPI implementation mechanisms have received little scholarly attention, especially in the context of distributed software product development. We took an action research approach to study the SPI journey of a large multinational enterprise that adopted a distributed product development strategy. We describe the interventions and action research cycles enacted over a period of five years in collaboration with the firm, which resulted in a custom SPI framework that catered to both the social and technical needs of the firm's distributed teams. Institutionalizing the process maturity framework got stalled initially because the SPI initiatives were perceived by product line managers as a mechanism for exercising wider controls by the firm's top management. The implementation mechanism was subsequently altered to co-opt product line managers, which contributed to a wider adoption of the SPI framework. Insights that emerge from our analysis of the firm's SPI journey pertain to the integration of the technical and social views of software development, preserving process diversity through the use of a multi-tiered, non-blueprint approach to SPI, the linkage between key process areas and project control modes, and the role of SPI in aiding organizational learning.\nPublished in:\nSoftware Engineering, IEEE Transactions on  (Volume:40 ,  Issue: 3 )\nDate of Publication:\nMarch 2014\n\nPage(s):\n    235 - 250 \nISSN :\n    0098-5589 \nINSPEC Accession Number:\n    14209936 \nDOI:\n    10.1109/TSE.2013.58 \n\nDate of Publication :\n    12 December 2013 \nDate of Current Version :\n    31 March 2014 \nIssue Date :\n    March 2014 \nSponsored by :\n    IEEE Computer Society \nPublisher:\n    IEEE \n\nMob: 9985613074, 7396708351\n\nSite: www.newieeeprojects.com\n\nEmail: newieeeprojects@gmail.com", "tags": ["IEEE", "IEEE projects 2014"], "title": "Governing Software Process Improvementsin Globally Distributed    | IEEE | IEEE projects 2014", "publishedAt": "2014-09-06T12:28:18Z", "channelTitle": "Renown Technologies"}, "contentDetails": {"duration": "PT1M24S"}}]}, {"idx_paper": "10.1109/TSE.2013.58", "q": "10.1109/TSE.2013.58", "items": [{"id": "J1T6FOptVpQ", "statistics": {"favoriteCount": "0", "commentCount": "1", "viewCount": "3493", "dislikeCount": "0", "likeCount": "10"}, "snippet": {"channelId": "UCXRQfQk2bC9OxldX0EmsWvw", "description": "Kinematics and kinetics of long-axis rotation in avian bipedal locomotion\r\nS.M. This vehicle is intended to be used as a testbed for evaluating distributed estimation and control algorithms. We present the custom hardware, dynamics model, method of state estimation, and a parameterized control strategy capable of controlling any flight-feasible configuration of the vehicle. In terms of the control strategy, we describe a method for optimizing its parameters in order to achieve the best possible performance subject to the system's physical constraints. We then show a straightforward method of mapping the configuration space of our vehicle to the control parameter space. Experimental results are included, demonstrating flight for a variety of configurations both indoors and outdoors.", "publishedAt": "2014-05-22T00:51:14Z", "channelTitle": "IJRR Multimedia"}, "contentDetails": {"duration": "PT1M9S"}}]}, {"idx_paper": "10.1177/0278364913501212", "q": "10.1177/0278364913501212", "items": [{"id": "j6328XoXXrc", "statistics": {"favoriteCount": "0", "viewCount": "476", "dislikeCount": "0", "likeCount": "1"}, "snippet": {"channelId": "UC937VH2K4sQgijIkuI3YEFw", "title": "The Distributed Flight Array (2 of 2)", "description": "\"The Distributed Flight Array: Design, implementation, and analysis of a modular vertical take-off and landing vehicle\"\nby Raymond Oung and Raffaello D'Andrea\n\nInternational Journal of Robotics Research 33(3): 375-400\nhttp://dx.doi.org/10.1177/0278364913501212\n\nWe describe the design and implementation of a modular vertical take-off and landing vehicle, which can be assembled and flown in an unlimited number of arbitrary configurations. This vehicle is intended to be used as a testbed for evaluating distributed estimation and control algorithms. We present the custom hardware, dynamics model, method of state estimation, and a parameterized control strategy capable of controlling any flight-feasible configuration of the vehicle. In terms of the control strategy, we describe a method for optimizing its parameters in order to achieve the best possible performance subject to the system's physical constraints. We then show a straightforward method of mapping the configuration space of our vehicle to the control parameter space. Experimental results are included, demonstrating flight for a variety of configurations both indoors and outdoors.", "publishedAt": "2014-05-22T00:49:31Z", "channelTitle": "IJRR Multimedia"}, "contentDetails": {"duration": "PT28S"}}]}, {"idx_paper": "10.1007/s10514-013-9339-y", "q": "10.1007/s10514-013-9339-y", "items": [{"id": "mI_14kzpGHQ", "statistics": {"favoriteCount": "0", "commentCount": "0", "viewCount": "44", "dislikeCount": "0", "likeCount": "0"}, "snippet": {"channelId": "UCR7Y71c9O9M9mZX0EkwEWGg", "description": "Slides from my PhD Defense, 2013.\n\nThe pdf manuscript is available from : http://nguyensmai.free.fr/thesislink.html\n\nSee the presenter's view on https://youtu.be/x2NkuS4xxw4\n*****\nShortcuts to the sections of the video:\n1. 1:42  Background : Life-Long Learning \n    (chapter 1.2 of the pdf)\n2. 3:14  Challenge : Sampling an Open-ended Environment \n    (chapter 1.3 of the pdf)\n3. 12:46  Idea : Data-collection Strategy based on Social Guidance and Artificial Curiosity \n    (chapter 2 of the pdf)\n4. 22:15 Realisation1 : Algorithmic Architecture to Learn Faster and with Better Precision\n    (chapter 3 of the pdf)\n5. 41:41 Realisation2 : Modeling and Understanding Child Development \n    (chapter 5 of the pdf)\n6. 44:06 Conclusion: Discover the Environment to Structure its Development \n    (chapter 6 of the pdf)\n\n*****\nAbstract:\nThe challenges posed by  robots operating in human environments on a daily basis and in the long-term point out the importance of adaptivity to changes which can be unforeseen at design time. The robot must learn continuously in an open-ended, non-stationary and high dimensional space. It must be able to know which parts to sample and what kind of skills are interesting to learn.  One way is to decide what to explore by oneself. Another way is to refer to a mentor. We name these two ways of collecting data \"sampling modes\". The first sampling mode correspond to algorithms  developed in the literature in order to autonomously drive the robot in interesting parts of the environment or useful kinds of skills. Such algorithms are called \"artificial curiosity\" or \"intrinsic motivation\" algorithms.  The second sampling mode  correspond to \"social guidance\" or \"imitation\" where the teacher indicates where to explore as well as where not to explore. Starting from the study of the relationships between these two concurrent methods, we ended up building  an algorithmic architecture with a hierarchical learning structure, called \"Socially Guided Intrinsic Motivation\" (SGIM).\n\n\nWe have built an intrinsically motivated active learner which learns how its actions can produce varied consequences or outcomes.  It actively learns online by sampling data which it chooses by using several sampling modes. On the meta-level, it actively learns which data collection strategy is most efficient for improving its competence and generalising from its experience to a wide variety of outcomes. The interactive learner thus learns multiple tasks in a structured manner, discovering by itself developmental sequences.\n\nThe study of the bootstrapping of autonomous exploration by social interaction with numerous users has led to algorithms such as :\n\n    SGIM-D (Socially Guided Intrinsic Motivation by Demonstration) which uses imitation learning and actively chooses which outcome to learn to produce based on intrinsic motivation. See Nguyen, Baranes and Oudeyer, 2011, ICDL.\n    SGIM-IM (Socially Guided Intrinsic Motivation with Interactive learning at the Meta level) which in addition actively chooses between an autonomous learning strategy or an imitation learning strategy. See Nguyen and Oudeyer, 2012, Humanoids.\n    SGIM-ACTS (Socially Guided Intrinsic Motivation with Active Choice of Teacher and Strategy) which chooses actively and hierarchichally at each learning episode what and how to learn, and when, what and who to imitate, depending on measures of competence progress that each learning strategy and teacher induces for different kinds of outcome. See Nguyen and Oudeyer, Paladyn Journal of Behavioral Robotics, DOI 10.2478/s13230-013-0110-z.\n\n*****\nReferences\n\nPhD Thesis (2013): A Curious Robot Learner for Interactive Goal-Babbling : In this strategy, as can be seen in the movie, the elephant stretched out and placed the tip of the trunk on the experimenter's forearm, proceeding to slide it along the forearm, hand, and along the length of the extended index finger before extrapolating the line of movement in the direction of the target container and successfully obtaining the reward...\n\n- Elephants Get the Point of Pointing, Study Shows\nhttp://www.nytimes.com/2013/10/10/science/elephants-get-the-point-of-pointing-study-shows.html?ref=science&_r=1&\n\nReference\nAfrican Elephants Can Use Human Pointing Cues to Find Hidden Food\nCurrent Biology, 10 October 2013 DOI: 10.1016/j.cub.2013.08.037  \nhttp://www.cell.com/current-biology/abstract/S0960-9822%2813%2901043-9\n\nHighlights\n- African elephants can use human pointing cues to find hidden food\n- Success is immediate, despite few prior learning opportunities\n- Pointing is followed even when human's body position conflicts with point direction\n- With visually subtle pointing, performance matches that of 2-year-old children\n\nSummary\nHow animals gain information from attending to the behavior of others has been widely studied, driven partly by the importance of referential pointing in human cognitive development, but species differences in reading human social cues remain unexplained. One explanation is that this capacity evolved during domestication, but it may be that only those animals able to interpret human-like social cues were successfully domesticated. Elephants are a critical taxon for this question: despite their longstanding use by humans, they have never been domesticated. Here we show that a group of 11 captive African elephants, seven of them significantly as individuals, could interpret human pointing to find hidden food. We suggest that success was not due to prior training or extensive learning opportunities. Elephants successfully interpreted pointing when the experimenter's proximity to the hiding place was varied and when the ostensive pointing gesture was visually subtle, suggesting that they understood the experimenter's communicative intent. The elephant's native ability in interpreting social cues may have contributed to its long history of effective use by man.", "tags": ["African Elephant (Organism Classification)", "Food (Industry)", "Loxodonta africana", "behavior", "human cognitive development"], "title": "African Elephants Can Use Human Pointing Cues to Find Hidden Food (Movie s4)", "publishedAt": "2013-10-10T18:31:01Z", "channelTitle": "Stefano Di Criscio"}, "contentDetails": {"duration": "PT9S"}}]}, {"idx_paper": "10.1016/j.ins.2013.08.037", "q": "10.1016/j.ins.2013.08.037", "items": [{"id": "NJaILHLSgfE", "statistics": {"favoriteCount": "0", "commentCount": "1", "viewCount": "6662", "dislikeCount": "1", "likeCount": "6"}, "snippet": {"channelId": "UCXGt8Ymi0WHsU-NogCuT9bg", "description": "Movie S3: Experimental Trial with E in an Asymmetric Position, Nearer to the Container on Her Left, Related to Figure 1. E points with the whole ipsilateral arm to the container on the right. The movie shows that the handler sometimes made a slight movement between the time the social cue was given and the elephants' choice. We tested whether the subjects still chose correctly when the handler's movement was neutral (directly forward, backward, or no movement at all) and thus not potentially helpful to the elephant in making its choice...\n\n- Elephants Get the Point of Pointing, Study Shows\nhttp://www.nytimes.com/2013/10/10/science/elephants-get-the-point-of-pointing-study-shows.html?ref=science&_r=1&\n\nReference\nAfrican Elephants Can Use Human Pointing Cues to Find Hidden Food\nCurrent Biology, 10 October 2013 DOI: 10.1016/j.cub.2013.08.037  \nhttp://www.cell.com/current-biology/abstract/S0960-9822%2813%2901043-9\n\nHighlights\n- African elephants can use human pointing cues to find hidden food\n- Success is immediate, despite few prior learning opportunities\n- Pointing is followed even when human's body position conflicts with point direction\n- With visually subtle pointing, performance matches that of 2-year-old children\n\nSummary\nHow animals gain information from attending to the behavior of others has been widely studied, driven partly by the importance of referential pointing in human cognitive development, but species differences in reading human social cues remain unexplained. One explanation is that this capacity evolved during domestication, but it may be that only those animals able to interpret human-like social cues were successfully domesticated. Elephants are a critical taxon for this question: despite their longstanding use by humans, they have never been domesticated. Here we show that a group of 11 captive African elephants, seven of them significantly as individuals, could interpret human pointing to find hidden food. We suggest that success was not due to prior training or extensive learning opportunities. Elephants successfully interpreted pointing when the experimenter's proximity to the hiding place was varied and when the ostensive pointing gesture was visually subtle, suggesting that they understood the experimenter's communicative intent. One explanation is that this capacity evolved during domestication, but it may be that only those animals able to interpret human-like social cues were successfully domesticated. Elephants are a critical taxon for this question: despite their longstanding use by humans, they have never been domesticated. Here we show that a group of 11 captive African elephants, seven of them significantly as individuals, could interpret human pointing to find hidden food. We suggest that success was not due to prior training or extensive learning opportunities. Elephants successfully interpreted pointing when the experimenter's proximity to the hiding place was varied and when the ostensive pointing gesture was visually subtle, suggesting that they understood the experimenter's communicative intent. Dogs show asymmetric tail-wagging responses to different emotive stimuli\u2014the outcome of different activation of left and right brain structures controlling tail movements to the right and left side of the body. A crucial question, however, is whether or not dogs detect this asymmetry. Here we report that dogs looking at moving video images of conspecifics exhibiting prevalent left- or right-asymmetric tail wagging showed higher cardiac activity and higher scores of anxious behavior when observing left- rather than right-biased tail wagging. JETem 2017", "description": "Patel A, et al. Pneumonia Diagnosed by Point-of-Care Ultrasound. JETem 2017. 2(3):V11-12. https://doi.org/10.21980/J8XP64\n\nHistory of present illness:  A 46-year-old female was sent to the emergency department from clinic with an oxygen saturation of 86%. She endorsed fevers with cough and generalized weakness for one week. She had been evaluated by her primary care provider on day two of illness and was started empirically on Oseltamivir without improvement of her symptoms. The patient arrived febrile, tachycardic, tachypneic, and hypoxic on room air with left-sided crackles on exam.\n\nSignificant findings: Point-of-care ultrasound of the left lower lobe demonstrates lung hepatization, a classic finding for pneumonia. In addition, a shred sign is present with both air bronchograms and focal B-lines\u2014all suggestive of poorly aerated, consolidated lung(1). The patient was started on antibiotics and admitted to the hospital with a diagnosis of community-acquired pneumonia.\n\nDiscussion: With a systematic approach, a skilled clinician can distinguish patterns on point-of-care ultrasound to evaluate for underlying pathology of the lung. Such findings include focal B-lines, an artifact that results from altered fluid-air compositions in the alveoli(2,3).\n\nStudies show, that when referenced with the gold standard of computed tomography, ultrasound is more sensitive than chest X-ray in diagnosing pneumonia. Nazerian et al(4). conducted a 190-patient study in which ultrasound had a higher sensitivity\u201481% compared to a dismal 65% by chest X-ray. Similarly, Cortellaro et al(5). concluded bedside ultrasound (96% sensitivity) is a reliable and superior tool for timely diagnosis of pneumonia compared to chest X-ray (69% sensitivity).\n\nBourcier et al(6). drew conclusions on the effectiveness of ultrasound over chest X-ray on two groups of patients. In the group who exhibited symptoms for less than 24 hours, ultrasound outperformed chest X-ray, where the PPV of ultrasound was 76% and chest X-ray 23%. For those who exhibited symptoms for more than 24 hours, ultrasound demonstrated a PPV of 93% while chest X-ray only 77%. Thus, the use of point-of-care ultrasound in the diagnosis of pneumonia can improve sensitivity while minimizing radiation. \n\nTopics: Pneumonia, ultrasound, dyspnea, shortness of breath, hypoxic, respiratory, pulmonology. \n\nReferences: \n1. Warm Shower or Bath\n\nTaking a warm shower or bath 1-2 hrs before bed can improve sleep quality. Mediterranean Diet(MD)\n\nMD has many benefits for our body including our brain health as well as preventing the risk of dementia. In the past decade, several assessment tools worldwide have been developed to evaluate the effectiveness of the increasing number of neighborhood (re)development plans. While there is a plethora of research investigating various aspects of building assessment tools, until now little studies have looked at the empirical aspects of neighborhood sustainability assessment. To address this lacuna in literature, this study explores the uptake of sustainability criteria in projects certified under neighborhood sustainability assessment tools. In addition, it examines three cases from the US, the UK, and Japan that have been highly ranked under LEED-ND, BREEAM Communities, and CASBEE-UD, respectively. This is done through investigating compliance of each case with the principles of sustainable development. Furthermore, a series of comparative case studies are undertaken to evaluate each case using assessment tools other than the ones that have, in reality, been used for certifying the selected developments. In addition to highlighting strengths and weaknesses of each development, results show that social, economic, and institutional aspects are not adequately accounted for in theory and practice. Practice of neighborhood sustainability assessment is, to a large extent, market-driven and characterized by the dominance of the environmental aspects of sustainability. Also, results indicate that assessment tools can co-evolve through learning from their successes and failures. FEARLUS-SPOMM is a coupled socio-ecosystem model in which land managers (the agents) must choose among a number of land uses (here divided into two abstract types (arable and grazing) each with three levels of intensity) that provide different amounts of habitat for one or more of ten species the distribution of which is computed using a stochastic patch occupancy metacommunity model. A government agent administers an agri-environmental incentive scheme to the land managers, with the goal of improving/maintaining biodiversity.\n\nIn the video, the top graph shows the history of choices made by the land manager agents, the middle shows the (stylised) market for different land uses, and the bottom graph shows the species distributions. On the right hand side, visualisations of the spatial distribution of land use choices (top) and species abundance (bottom) are also shown. It then shows the results of 20 runs using the same scenario settings but different values for the seed of the pseudo-random number generator, before showing (very rapidly) similar results from all the scenarios of incentive scheme design, incentive amount, market variability, input costs and land manager aspirations that are explored in the above paper. Here, there are four graphs -- the top shows a time series of the land use choices made (and corresponds to the top graph in the earlier part of the video), the second shows the market, the third shows government expenditure, and the fourth various measures of biodiversity.\n\nThe goal of the video is to highlight the computational demands entailed in exploring parameter spaces of models of this kind, and in analysing the results thereof. It was presented at a Discussion Workshop on Sensitivity Analysis of Agent-Based Models at Wageningen UR on 19 June 2013. For example, meaning is attributed to a single \"concept center\", \"semantic hub\", or \"lexico-semantic binding site\". This approach runs into difficulty because research has documented a wide range of cortical areas relevant to meaning processes; the idea of a single semantic hub seems, therefore, insufficient. In addition, aphasia resulting from a focal cortical lesion typically involves all language modalities -- including the production and understanding of speech -- thus arguing against a subdivision of language function into local cortical modules and, instead, suggesting the integrated processing of motor and sensory aspects of language.\nNeurobiologically founded brain-language theory uses principles known from neuroscience research underlying the structure and function of the nervous system in order to deduce and explain language functions. Such neuroscience principles and knowledge include information about neuroanatomical connectivity -- for example the strong left-hemispheric dorsal connection between frontal and temporal areas by way of the arcuate fascicle -- and information about neurophysiological activation and resultant neuronal plasticity -- for example 'Hebb's rule,' according to which neurons that fire together wire together and therefore correlated neuronal activation is mapped into strongly connected structural circuits.\nA theoretical foundation of neurobiological brain language models is provided by neurocomputational research. Neural architectures incorporate neuroanatomical structures relevant for language and meaning processing along with their connectivity in the human brain. Neuronal function is incorporated in these models by mimicking nerve cell dynamics, activation and inhibition, global and local regulation of activity and neural plasticity according to Hebbian plasticity principles. These realistic neurobiological models tend to build DNAs as a consequence of correlated sensory and motor activation. Many of these DNAs are action perception circuits because they connect sensory and motor neurons into sensorimotor 'mirror' representations. Different activation states of DNAs can be linked to cognitive processes such as word and object recognition, comprehension, action performance, memory, attention, intention, decision, combination and prediction. At the linguistic level, phonological, lexical and morphological, syntactic, semantic and social-pragmatic functions can be modeled. As the neurocomputational models are concrete, in terms of brain areas and temporal dynamics, they allow for novel predictions on to-be-performed new experiments using neuroimaging, neuropsychological and neurophysiological methods. The interplay between theoretical and experimental brain language research opens fruitful explanatory research perspectives for the future5.\n1              Pulverm\u00fcller, F. [ http://www.ingber.com/smni14_eeg_ca.pdf or  http://dx.doi.org/10.1016/j.jtbi.2013.11.002 ]", "tags": ["Smni"], "title": "EEG Influence on Ca Waves", "publishedAt": "2013-11-17T03:59:22Z", "channelTitle": "Lester Ingber"}, "contentDetails": {"duration": "PT5M10S"}}]}, {"idx_paper": "10.1016/j.im.2013.11.007", "q": "10.1016/j.im.2013.11.007", "items": [{"id": "iFeXQNmQS6A", "statistics": {"favoriteCount": "0", "commentCount": "0", "viewCount": "78", "dislikeCount": "0", "likeCount": "1"}, "snippet": {"channelId": "UCD9mrrOEFGGiRuZJKJ-gaGQ", "description": "The published paper is available here: http://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0304388613001460\n\nDOI: 10.1016/j.elstat.2013.11.007", "tags": ["Thomson Problem", "Mathematics", "Smale Problem", "Electrostatics", "Science", "Physics", "Periodic Table", "Electron", "Shell-filling", "Point Groups"], "title": "Discrete Charge Transformations in the Thomson Problem", "publishedAt": "2014-03-06T23:32:33Z", "channelTitle": "Tim LaFave Jr"}, "contentDetails": {"duration": "PT5M3S"}}]}, {"idx_paper": "10.1016/j.im.2013.11.007", "q": "10.1016/j.im.2013.11.007", "items": [{"id": "1eWXqkFDTQM", "statistics": {"favoriteCount": "0", "commentCount": "20", "viewCount": "2752", "dislikeCount": "1", "likeCount": "105"}, "snippet": {"channelId": "UC4YDcxhBMxyaYf04dYzPpYw", "description": "Die Hornotter (Vipera ammodytes) wird vielfach auch als die giftigste Festlandschlange Europas bezeichnet. Das Gift ist relativ stark. Es wirkt haupts\u00e4chlich auf das Blut- und Gef\u00e4\u00dfsystem, indem es die W\u00e4nde der Blutgef\u00e4\u00dfe zerst\u00f6rt und die Gerinnung des Blutes unterbindet. Zudem wirkt es auch noch neurotoxisch. Der Biss der Hornotter ist sehr schmerzhaft und f\u00fchrt zu lokalen R\u00f6tungen und Schwellungen. Schwei\u00dfausbr\u00fcche, Bauchschmerzen, \u00dcbelkeit und Erbrechen k\u00f6nnen hinzukommen. Auch neurologische Symptome, wie zum Beispiel Atemnot sind nicht auszuschlie\u00dfen. Das Gift beinhaltet zahlreiche Proteine und Peptide. Zu den wichtigsten geh\u00f6ren die Phospholipasen A2, Serinproteasen und Metalloproteasen. Ammodytin L ist eine myotoxische Phospholipase A2. Das Enzym greift die Muskulatur an und zerst\u00f6rt Muskelzellen (Krizaj et al. 1991). Auf dem Gift von Vipera ammodytes wurden unter anderen zwei Metalloproteasen mit dem Name VaH1 und VaH2 isoliert. Metalloproteasen wirken h\u00e4morrhagisch, indem sie die Blutgef\u00e4\u00dfe perphorieren (Leonardi et al. 2002). Dadurch kommt es zu R\u00f6tungen an der Bissstelle, die sich auf die gebissene Extremit\u00e4t ausbreiten k\u00f6nnen. Au\u00dferdem findet man im Gift von Vipera ammodytes auch noch Serinproteasen. Diese Enzyme sind daf\u00fcr bekannt, dass sie gr\u00f6\u00dftenteils mit dem Blutgerinnungssystem interagieren. Eine Serinprotease mit dem Name VaSP1 zeigt eine Aktivit\u00e4t gegen verschiedene Blutgerinnungsfaktoren und verhindert die Blutgerinnung (Kurtovi\u0107 et al. 2014). Disintegrine im Gift der Schlange verhindern die Aggregation von Thrombozyten und wirken dadurch ebenfalls der Blutgerinnung entgegen (Calvete et al. 2003). Eine der wichtigsten Komponenten im Gift von Vipera ammodytes ist Ammodytoxin A. Hierbei handelt es sich um ein pr\u00e4synaptisches Neurotoxin, welches zu einer irreversibelen L\u00e4hmung f\u00fchrt (Ritonja und Gubenek 1985). Wie Ammodytoxin genau wirkt ist noch unklar.\nIn Versuchen konnte gezeigt werden, dass es mit Komponenten aus den Mitochondrien der Zelle interagiert. In den Mitochondrien der Zellen an der motorischen Endplatte befindet sich ein Rezeptor, mit dem Name R25. R25 stellt eine Untereinheit der Cytochrome-C-Oxidase dar. Die CCOX ist ein wichtiger Bestandteil der Atmungskette in den Mitochondrien. Durch die Bindung von Ammodytoxin an dem Rezeptor scheint die Atmungskette beeintr\u00e4chtigt zu werden, was zu einem Mangel an ATP in der Zelle f\u00fchrt. Durch die Wirkung von Ammodytoxin wird die Aussch\u00fcttung von Neurotransmittern an der motorischen Endplatte verhindert (\u0160ribar et al.). Abgesehen davon, wirkt es auch noch hemmend auf die Blutgerinnung, indem es den Faktor Xa bindet. Faktor Xa ist eine der wichtigsten Komponenten in der Gerinnungskaskade (Prijatelj et al. 2006). Aus Vipera ammodytes meridionalis, einer Unterart, die haupts\u00e4chlich in Griechenland verbreitet ist, konnte man ein postsynaptisches Neurotoxin mit dem Name Vipoxin isolieren (Atanasov et al. 2012). \n\nQuellen \nAtanasov, Vasil N.; Stoykova, Silviya; Goranova, Yana; Mitewa, Mariana; Petrova, Svetla (2012): Acute toxicity of vipoxin and its components. Land Trust to develop a meme campaign. The campaign focused on promoting Kestrel\u2019s lands to get people, particularly students, to get outdoors. Getting people outdoors and enjoying the environment is an important part of becoming more sustainable, as experiencing nature leads to caring about it more.  We hiked two of Kestrel\u2019s conserved lands around Amherst, Sweet Alice and Mount Pollux. We took pictures on these hikes to use for our memes. We then met as a group to develop memes for Kestrel using an online design software. These memes included messages such as \u201cNeed a day at the Beech? Visit Sweet Alice Conservation Area!\u201d These memes will be posted by Kestrel to their Facebook page and other social media platforms. This was an incredibly rewarding experience for all of us.
